# moving data from one place to another in a text file

_211 messages · 18 participants · 2005-02 → 2005-03_

---

### moving data from one place to another in a text file

- **From:** "francesco" <fontana.bleu@libero.it>
- **Date:** 2005-02-14T04:02:41-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
I have a COBOL file that reads an entire text file and gives as an
output the same input file with another name. The problem is that in
the input file the last two lines (that contain numerical values only)
must be located some place else in the output file. How can I achieve
this ?

Thank you

Francesco
```

#### ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

- **From:** l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms)
- **Date:** 2005-02-14T13:15:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9Qs7Nf6uflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
.    On  14.02.05
  wrote  fontana.bleu@libero.it (francesco)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com
  about  moving data from one place to another in a text file


fb> I have a COBOL file that reads an entire text file and gives as an
fb> output the same input file with another name. The problem is that in
fb> the input file the last two lines (that contain numerical values
fb> only) must be located some place else in the output file. How can I
fb> achieve this ?

   Is it know beforehand, where in the file these last two lines have  
to be inserted? Both in one location or at different ones?

   If yes, just split the file at those places where these two lines  
have to be inserted. Write all records to the output file, until you  
have reached the place where the newly inserted have to go, divert the  
rest of the file into a temporary file, then write those two  
insertables, and copy the temporary file to the actual outpï¿½ut file.


Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.willms-edv.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

Gerade das Gegenteil tun heiï¿½t auch nachahmen, es heiï¿½t nï¿½mlich das Gegenteil nachahmen. -G.C.Lichtenberg
```

#### ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-14T10:22:37-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108405357.508058.199130@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
> the last two lines (that contain numerical values only)
> must be located some place else in the output file.

Read through the file and store each line in two buffers in
working-storage such that when the 'at end' is reached these are the
last two lines.

ie

|    perform until endoffile
|        read infile at end set endoffile
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
|    end-perform

then restart the file reading and writing inserting the two records
where required and dropping them when read.
```

#### ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-16T11:20:02+00:00

```
On 14 Feb 2005 04:02:41 -0800, "francesco" <fontana.bleu@libero.it>
wrote:

>I have a COBOL file that reads an entire text file and gives as an
>output the same input file with another name. The problem is that in
>the input file the last two lines (that contain numerical values only)
>must be located some place else in the output file. How can I achieve
>this ?

1. Read the file into a linked list. 
2. Re-link the last two lines into their new location. 
3. Write the list to an output file.
```

- **References:** `<1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <vqa6115uro7gku7ggl32oq08an1mrp8frp@4ax.com> <9Q-RodD9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <q3d9111hf8n3l7tuel1irc31l1i0slb5mf@4ax.com>`

```
On Thu, 17 Feb 2005 19:47:41 -0500, Binyamin Dissen
<postingid@dissensoftware.com> wrote:

>On 16 Feb 2005 17:02:00 GMT l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) wrote:
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>The "linkage" need not be addresses.

Correct, it can be done with subscripts .. at the cost of wasting
memory by padding each line with spaces and limiting the maximum
number of lines to some arbitrary number. 

Why not do it right? Should we forever be limited by a mediocre
programmer who left in the '70s .. or hung around and is now about to
reture?

linkage section.
01  line-entry.
     05  next-line     pointer.
     05  text-length binary pic s9(04).
     05  line-text.
           10   occurs 1 to 1000 depending on text-length pic x.

procedure division.

    open input-file
    perform read-input
    perform until end-of-file
     compute alloc-length = input-length
           + length of next-line + length of text-length
     call 'malloc' using by value alloc-length 
                      returning  next-line
*> per the 2002 Standard the above becomes
*> allocate alloc-length returning next-line
      set address of line-entry to next-line
      set next-line to null
      move input-length to text-length
      move input-text to line-text
      perform read-input
    end-perform
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

- **References:** `<1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <vqa6115uro7gku7ggl32oq08an1mrp8frp@4ax.com> <9Q-RodD9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <q3d9111hf8n3l7tuel1irc31l1i0slb5mf@4ax.com> <6via11hg4sd1k5c3mv33pn8tmusgfdga2l@4ax.com>`

```
> Should we forever be limited by a mediocre programmer who left in the
'70s ..

No, we should follow your example and do it like C programmers did in
the 80s, before they discovered modern programming.

Your code may well show how clever you think you are by changing the
problem into a different problem, but it does not actually solve what
was required.  The data started as a serial set of lines in a file and
is now a serial set of lines in a list.

An actual solution may be:

       open infile
       until eof:
           read infile
                 at end set eof
                 not at end
                       move b to a
                       move inrec to b
           end-read
       close infile

       unset eof
       open infile
       open outfile
       until eof:
            read infile
                  at end set eof
                  not at end
                  if inrec = a or b )
                       continue
                  else
                      if ( this is where to insert )
                           write outrec from a
                           write outrec from b
                      end-if
                      write outrec from inrec
                  end-if
              end-read
         close infile
         close outfile

Why complicate it with irrelevant stuff.
```

```
The general solution to this class of problem is called a Text Editor.
On Unix, it could be scripted with SED or AWK. Since the request was
for a Cobol solution, I provided a generalized one: convert the file
to a list and manipulate the lines therein. That's what text editors
do. It's cleaner than your solution of reading the file twice.
```

```
Pointer syntax is the same on MF, IBM and Fujitsu. That covers more
than 90% of compilers in use.

On others, a pointer can be dereferenced with CALL BY VALUE into a
program that receives it BY REFERENCE.

Most mainframe shops have an assembly language version.
```

```
.    On  19.02.05
  wrote  riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  1108877884.885222.23270@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com
  about  Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file


>> Pointer syntax is the same on MF, IBM and Fujitsu.

   I have no clues if that is true, but regarding this one:

r> As I may have mentioned before: Fujitsu don't have that usage
r> documented, it isn't in their syntax definition, it isn't supported.

   May I quote from the "Language Reference Manual" for Fujitsu COBOL  
3.0, the free student edition, which one can be download (the  
documentation independently from the compiler)
   from http://www.adtools.com/student/index.htm#3

--------- schnipp -----------------------------------------

106 Chapter 2. COBOL Modules

Pointer Handling

Use pointers for referencing the address area.

The address of an area can be found using the ADDR function
and set in the pointer data item. A pointer data item defines the
data description entry written in the USAGE IS POINTER clause.

You define the structure attributes of the area to be referenced
using a pointer in the BASED-STORAGE SECTION of the DATA
DIVISION. The data item area defined in the BASED-STORAGE
SECTION cannot be reserved.

Clauses can be described both in the data description entry of the
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION and also the BASED ON clause
written in the data description entry of the BASED-STORAGE
SECTION. Write the BASED ON clause to specify an implicit
pointer.

When referencing a data item defined in the BASED-STORAGE
SECTION, a pointer qualifier or an implicit pointer must be
added to the data-name.

------------------ schnapp --------------------------------


>> That covers more than 90% of compilers in use.

r> Please provide the data that shows this to be the case.  Or did you
r> just pull these figures out your arse and you seem to prefer to do.

   Fujitsu is 100% of the COBOL compilers which I do use...


Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.willms-edv.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

Diese ganze Lehre taugt zu nichts als darï¿½ber zu disputieren. -G.C.Lichtenberg
```

```
Don't confuse 'endorsed' with 'suported'. Based on what I've read,
Fujitsu Support is an oxymoron.

Interesting to note that the 2002 Standard does reluctantly endorse
the old syntax:

SET ADDRESS OF dataname TO pointer

It requires, however, that dataname be 'based'. Thus, the above is
effectively: 

SET basedpointer TO pointer

Still, the retro syntax will help conversion of old 'industry
standard' programs. (If we ever get around to converting them.)
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-02-23T08:36:31-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cvibef$127f$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<vqa6115uro7gku7ggl32oq08an1mrp8frp@4ax.com> <9Q-RodD9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <q3d9111hf8n3l7tuel1irc31l1i0slb5mf@4ax.com> <6via11hg4sd1k5c3mv33pn8tmusgfdga2l@4ax.com> <1108698281.178522.63150@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <1108703326.879934.273560@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <vmad111l0h49nmhnh1vcb6mp0gjioki38e@4ax.com> <p2dSd.2905172$f47.522482@news.easynews.com> <cvg5r1$2lgf$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3dpn119ol03uumjqlrj4tctk118pda6lpf@4ax.com>`

```
My honest opinion is that "operating environments" are almost always better
at managing memory and other system resources than the end-user programmer .
I am less than enamored with ALLOCATE / FREE and would suggest that they be
used sparingly.  That's actually a side point.

Your position seems to have been that this is a *general* solution
applicable *today* using *existing* compilers.  Which *available* compilers
are compliant with ISO/IEC 1989:2002?

I denigrate a feature of ISO/IEC 1989:2002 for *current* compilers and
*current* environments because the overwhelming majority of them are
compliant with ANSI X3.23-1985, and it is not yet clear to me that all
implementations conforming to that standard approach the subject of dynamic
memory using either the same semantics or the same syntax, *if at all*.   A
"general solution" requires *general availability*, not merely *availability
in the environments I find interesting*.  And "general availability" results
from "customer demand".  Don't blame the *vendors* for failing to provide a
feature of the current COBOL standard in a compiler compliant with a prior
one if the *customers* don't ask for it.  The same is true of *vendors*
electing not to produce a compiler fully-compliant with all specifications
of ISO/IEC 1989:2002; it's an expensive proposition, and investing large
sums of money in a new product with little hope of recouping that investment
through sales of that product is not generally considered good business
practice.

Why do I denigrate a solution provided in ISO/IEC 1989:2002?  Because that
solution is not available as a standard feature in most standards-compliant
COBOL implementations today, given that most of them conform to ANSI
X3.23-1985 (and some are still marketed that conform to ANSI X3.23-1974).  A
solution that does not provide exactly the same results with exactly the
same syntax in all implementations is not a "general" solution.

    -Chuck Stevens

"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3dpn119ol03uumjqlrj4tctk118pda6lpf@4ax.com...
```

```
On Thu, 24 Feb 2005 03:18:05 GMT, "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message 
>news:2dbq1198emt1p5r3827lfgskmhl90m1bn3@4ax.com...
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>generalized solution for?

I was referring to the general problem of lists. Given that two lines
need to be moved, they can be stored in a two entry array and the
input file read twice. A general version of the problem would call for
moving m lines, where m can be zero thru the file size (n). An example
is sorting the file. Sorting could be done with a two entry array, but
the result would, on average, require copying n^2 records (assuming
one input and output file per pass).
```

- **References:** `<1108698281.178522.63150@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <1108703326.879934.273560@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <vmad111l0h49nmhnh1vcb6mp0gjioki38e@4ax.com> <p2dSd.2905172$f47.522482@news.easynews.com> <cvg5r1$2lgf$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3dpn119ol03uumjqlrj4tctk118pda6lpf@4ax.com> <cvibef$127f$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <2dbq1198emt1p5r3827lfgskmhl90m1bn3@4ax.com> <NHbTd.3222897$f47.578404@news.easynews.com> <375r11l7lpdk0v9rl93l1sv2pmnv3pithf@4ax.com>`

```
Personally, I would say that the "read twice" solution is the ONLY truly 
practical solution - as any solution requiring "memory allocation" canNOT handle 
potentially "available memory-size + 1" sized file input - while the read twice 
method can.

And, of course, the "read twice method" would be portable to EVERY ANSI/ISO 
conforming compiler from the '68 Standard on.

As with the cross-language challenge, if a "real world" solution were required 
to be the "fastest" possible *AND* the size of the input file were smaller than 
the "probable" available memory, then an "in memory solution" could be created 
(but that could also use an ANSI/ISO Standard table - or an ANSI/ISO Standard 
SORT - or any of a number of "practical" and PORTABLE solutions)
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8kns11hej65j93o4p3kk1g71f71li5ebe9@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<1108703326.879934.273560@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <vmad111l0h49nmhnh1vcb6mp0gjioki38e@4ax.com> <p2dSd.2905172$f47.522482@news.easynews.com> <cvg5r1$2lgf$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3dpn119ol03uumjqlrj4tctk118pda6lpf@4ax.com> <cvibef$127f$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <2dbq1198emt1p5r3827lfgskmhl90m1bn3@4ax.com> <NHbTd.3222897$f47.578404@news.easynews.com> <375r11l7lpdk0v9rl93l1sv2pmnv3pithf@4ax.com> <1109269490.351368.158610@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 24 Feb 2005 10:24:50 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> A general version of the problem would call for
>> moving m lines, where m can be zero thru the file size (n)
>
>'Zero' and 'file size' means no lines moved.  In fact the maximum of
>this specific type of problem is 'half the file'.

When output order is the reverse of input order, the number of lines
moved is n - 1. When records are shuffled like a deck of cards, the
number moved is n (unless you're planning to 'deal bottoms').

>> An example is sorting the file.
>
>The general solution to 'sorting the file' is sorting.

Sorting is a special case of Reordering Objects. The fastest way to
reorder objects in an ordered collection is to exchange pointers, as
opposed to physically moving the objects.
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

> What are we practitioners supposed to do while the Standards process

What five-year hiatus?  For the features you've specified, the *standards*
process is done.

> My solution is to use syntax that works the same on IBM, Micro Focus
> and Fujitsu.

Goody.  It is not a *general* solution if it makes use of
*implementor-defined* extensions.

> I avoid the ALLOCATE verb, available on Micro Focus and
> the '02 Standard, because it isn't available on the other two.

If it isn't available on the other two, it's because they don't conform (or

extensions to standard COBOL?

COBOL74, and to try to get our customer base to move to COBOL85 we made it
quite a bit more expensive.  Thus far it hasn't worked.

A *general* solution works in all COBOL environments.  If it doesn't work
in, or even isn't appropriate for, *all* COBOL environments, it fails the
test of being a *general* solution.

Or are you changing the qualifier from "general" to "PRACTICAL" on us?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 21)_

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-27T11:12:48-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f1t321l54qf3vl7fguh8l9j3gef9o21u5h@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<p2dSd.2905172$f47.522482@news.easynews.com> <jcps1157v8lrkd7rh1dg8k2bt761a939kg@4ax.com> <1109297999.928079.94980@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <pklv11to0ulse8is31lsqrgqbok8e5cicf@4ax.com> <cvpr4c$5ga$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```
On 26 Feb 2005 07:47:08 -0500, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>In article <pklv11to0ulse8is31lsqrgqbok8e5cicf@4ax.com>,
>Robert Wagner  <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>This seems to be a Brooklyn Bridge defense, Mr Wagner.

Brooklyn Bridge is Argumentum Ad Populum -- the greater the number of
people believing a proposition, the more likely it is to be true. I
didn't claim mainframers are in the majority. They're not.

>>A
>>month ago, their dogmatism and intolerance cost me $5K.
>
>So you find their actions to be so Good and Noble that you imitate them... 
>mainframers do (x) so you do it, as well?

On the contrary, my skepticism about (x) is increased.

>>My rants are not abstract; they're based on real life.
>
>They may be based on *a* life - yours - certainly enough... but one might 
>hope there's a bit more to the world than what is found in the small set 
>of accidents which define a given existence.

In logic, The Fallacy of Accident is committed when a general rule is
applied to a particular case whose "accidental" circumstances mean
that the rule is inapplicable.  It is the error made when one goes
from the general to the specific. 

You allege the opposite: The Fallacy of Converse Accident or Hasty
Generalization, wherein one forms a general rule by examining only a
few specific cases.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 22)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com
- **Date:** 2005-02-28T09:17:01-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cvv94t$4rh$1@panix5.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<p2dSd.2905172$f47.522482@news.easynews.com> <pklv11to0ulse8is31lsqrgqbok8e5cicf@4ax.com> <cvpr4c$5ga$1@panix5.panix.com> <f1t321l54qf3vl7fguh8l9j3gef9o21u5h@4ax.com>`

```
In article <f1t321l54qf3vl7fguh8l9j3gef9o21u5h@4ax.com>,
Robert Wagner  <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:
>On 26 Feb 2005 07:47:08 -0500, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>people believing a proposition, the more likely it is to be true.

Not quite, Mr Wagner.  The Brooklyn Bridge argument is an attempt to 
justify one's own behavior based on similar or identical behaviors of 
others... or *other*, and note the singular.  It applies equally well to:

'But everyone *else* is (doing (x))!'

'So everyone else jumps off the Brooklyn Bridge... you will, too?'

... and this applies almost to the Argumentum you cite.  However, consider 
the cases of

'But Jimmy, Janey, Billy and Barbara are (doing (x))!'...

... and ...

'But Kristen is (doing (x))!'

... and the response ending with '... jumps off the Brooklyn Bridge... you 
will, to?'

>I
>didn't claim mainframers are in the majority. They're not.

Nobody said you made such a claim, Mr Wagner, but in that you responded to 
an evaluation of your own behavior ('you insulted...') with an example of 
behavior by others ('I have been attacked') you appear to indulge in a 
Brooklyn Bridge defense.

>
>>>A
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>On the contrary, my skepticism about (x) is increased.

That makes your responding to evaluations of your own behavior with 
examples from their even more... curious.

'I do (y)... and if you ask about it I'll point out that the people I am 
skeptical of do (y), too!'

>
>>>My rants are not abstract; they're based on real life.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>few specific cases. 

I state, Mr Wagner, that what is based on '*a* life - yours -' (emphasis 
original) might be based on what is possibly a subset of what is to be 
found in the world... nothing more, nothing less.  If the small set of 
accidents which make up your single existence are the totality of that 
which is 'the world' then, it would seem, I am do well to re-evaluate my 
statement.

DD
```

- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <1108703326.879934.273560@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <vmad111l0h49nmhnh1vcb6mp0gjioki38e@4ax.com> <p2dSd.2905172$f47.522482@news.easynews.com> <cvg5r1$2lgf$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3dpn119ol03uumjqlrj4tctk118pda6lpf@4ax.com> <cvibef$127f$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <2dbq1198emt1p5r3827lfgskmhl90m1bn3@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 24 Feb 2005 11:20:03 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:


>RP>> Whereas your 'solution' reads the file once and then needs to
>'read'
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>non-solution.

A benchmark isn't required to determine thousands of instructions, in
the file system, take longer than several inline instructions. 

On the other hand, I've said many abuses during the Good Old Days
occurred because programmers were forced to hold the hand of
underpowered hardware. Now that we have CPU cycles to burn, we should
do right rather than sacrifice style to necessity. Put everything in a
database and don't worry about speed.

On the other other hand, I have weekly jobs that run 24 hours, during
which they max out 16 CPUs. That's excessive. All they're doing is
'database stuff' on millions of rows. We discover we can speed up
('optimize')  the jobs by partitioning the database and modifying the
APPLICATION to run parallel processes. If parm=low, process keys
beginning with 0-4; if parm=high, process keys beginning with 5-9.
Shades of the Good Old Days. Applications are back to holding the
platform's hand. The only difference is the platform has become the
database.

Plus CA Change, Plus C'Est La Meme Chose.
```

```
next record from a file takes thousands of instructions.

Data will be in a cache; instructions will not be.

Both are true. CPUs are idle six days a week and maxed out on the
```

```
On Sat, 26 Feb 2005 11:47:42 +0000, Jeff York <ralf4@btinternet.com>
wrote:

>Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pentium performs about as well as "Supercalc" used to on an 8MHz 286?

That's a good example because I worked on a spreadsheet like, but
faster than, Supercalc. Its speed came from hand-crafted Intel
assembly language. Porting to another hardware platform (Casio Boss)
required a complete rewrite. Porting to another OS, Win95, required a
major rewrite of the user interface. As complexity increased, adding
features became more and more difficult. 

The bloat in products like Excel free it from the stagnation of
platform and shallow product architecture. It eliminates the hidden
linkages found in 'small' software by adding speed-absorbing layers of
abstraction. It speeds up Time To Market and extends the useful life
of the product.
```

```
>API can be said to contain much in the way of abstraction.

The NT kernel is isolated from hardware by a Hardware Abstraction
Layer (HAL). NT 3 & 4 have run on DEC Alpha, MIPS, PowerPC, SPARC and
(rumored) IBM mainframe. That means Office and other applications
written to the NT API ran on those platforms.

Microsoft stopped targeting non-Intel processors in 1999 due to lack
of market demand. At the time, they had W2K running on Alpha and were
about to release it.

>>.. It speeds up Time To Market and extends the useful life
>>of the product. 
>
>"Useful life"??  It's revamped every ten minutes.  Rarely compatibly.
>:-)

Thanks for confirming my assertion about Time To Market. :)
```

```
On 27 Feb 2005 20:18:32 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> NT 3 & 4 have run on ... (rumored) IBM mainframe.
>
>There is/was NT for AS/400 which may loosely be called an IBM
>Mainframe, but it ran on an Intel CPU card plugged into the AS/400.

I worked on that machine in 1997. It was a kludge at the time. Two
incompatible systems housed in the same box. They shared a hard disk
by partitioning, but couldn't read each other's files. I've heard that
problem was solved later. The PC motherboard was way overpriced at
about $50K. 

An earlier version used the Novell operating system on the PC. 

Anyone wanting that configuration could have saved money by using a
regular server sharing files with the AS/400 via SMB and/or ODBC.
```

- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```
On 26 Feb 2005 07:50:01 -0500, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>In article <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com>,
>Robert Wagner  <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>'abuses' *except in the hindsight provided by decades of change*?

Take the simple example of Y2K. During the '80s, mainframers were
still holding  hardware's hand by 'saving' two bytes of memory while
ignoring the onrush of 2000. All the money they 'saved' in forty years
was consumed many times over by work spent correcting their abuse.

The idea of parallel processing by passing messages originated during
the '60s, in LISP, Smalltalk and Simula. The idea was largely ignored
by commercial EDP, with the notable exception of the brokerage
industry. During the '70s and '80s, I wasn't a fan of those languages
either, but was wrestling with solutions involving pipes.

The idea became popular around 1990, when it was marketed as OO/C++.
The marketing emphasis then was on program organization, not parallel
processing. However, it happened just when powerful processors such as
386 and 486 were being introduced.  I think the timing was no
coincidence. Now, fifteen years later, with speed and parallelism
galore, single-threaded solutions are a waste of resources. 

Why were they abusive in the Good Old Days, when we had a single CPU?
Because, if we'd had more vision, our solutions would have remained
useful through the '90s. There would have been no need for
'remediation' by rewriting in OO.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 19)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-27T15:10:34-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com>`

```
>> *except in the hindsight provided by decades of change*?

> All the money they 'saved' in forty years was consumed many
> times over by work spent correcting their abuse.

Please support this with the _evidence_ that you claim to always use.

Itemise the actual savings in each year (for example number of mag
times used, disk pack and drive usage, memory savings, card punch time
and card costs, time used in writing extra data to tape, and so on) and
convert to a common baseline. Itemise the actual Y2K costs in the same
baseline.

And don't do this for just one individual site but for all sites that
used Cobol, including those for which there was zero Y2K costs, and
including those systems which were replaced before, or even long
before, Y2K.

Otherwise will will consider that your 'many times over' is just
something you made up (which is exactly what it was).

> The idea became popular around 1990, when it was marketed as OO/C++.
> The marketing emphasis then was on program organization, not parallel
> processing.

That is because there is no relationship between parallel processing
and OO.  OO can be PP or or it need not.  PP maybe OO or it need not.

It happens that Simula solved the need for quasi-parallel processing in
a way that resulted in OO.  This does not mean that OO is parallel
processing or that they require each other.

> However, it happened just when powerful processors such as
> 386 and 486 were being introduced.

C++ was developed in 1981 and was commonly available (cflow) on Unix in
the 80s.  Compilers required more memory than an 8088 PC typically had
and so had to wait for larger memory machines to be available on PCs.

> I think the timing was no coincidence.

Well, duh. When the compiler requires more memory than an 8088 can
provide it is _not_ a 'co-incidence' that those compilers arrive with
the 386/486, it is a dependency.

> Now, fifteen years later, with speed and parallelism
> galore, single-threaded solutions are a waste of resources.

Servers are multi-CPU and clustered in order to run large numbers of
processes and are primarily aimed at server tasks, such as databases,
web-servers and app-servers.

> single-threaded solutions are a waste of resources.

'Single-threaded solutions' are only a waste of resources where those
resources are actually wasted.  A typical batch processing stream is
inherently sequential.  Where there may be benefit from overlap it is
typically obtained by using multiple batch streams, as has been done
for 40 years or more.

Interactive systems that I have created use multi-tasking to share
resources between many users, each being able to use many tasks.

There may be applications which can actually benefit from
multi-threading, but 'creating 800,000 threads one-per-word' is not one
of them, nor are many of the other example you claimed, such as having
one thread open a file, another read it and a third close it.  It won't
work.

It is certainly true that interactive games running on games consoles
can benefit from multi-threading on multple CPUs and GPUs.

> during the '60s, in LISP, Smalltalk and Simula. The idea was largely
ignored
> by commercial EDP,
> During the '70s and '80s, I wasn't a fan of those languages either,

I have serious doubts that you had even heard of them until much more
recently.
```

```
Experts cannot produce reasonable cost estimates. They range from $50B
by the US Securities and Exchange Commission to $300B by the Gartner
Group. The truth is probably around $200B. 

IBM's revenues between 1960 and 2000 were about $3,000B. If their
income could have been increased 1% by the use of four digit year, the
cost would have been $30B. 

The shortfall is $170B, about six times over.

By 2000, a significant percentage of business processing had moved to
non-mainframe (non-Cobol) platforms, where there was NO Y2K problem.
If mainframes had remained dominant, the cost could well have been
close to $1T.

>> However, it happened just when powerful processors such as

>
>C++ was developed in 1981 and was commonly available (cflow) on Unix in
>the 80s. 

The correct date is 1983.

> Compilers required more memory than an 8088 PC typically had
>and so had to wait for larger memory machines to be available on PCs.

By the late '80s, most PCs had well over one meg accessible via EMS.
Real memory was no limit on virtual memory systems.

>'Single-threaded solutions' are only a waste of resources where those
>resources are actually wasted.  A typical batch processing stream is
>inherently sequential.  Where there may be benefit from overlap it is
>typically obtained by using multiple batch streams, as has been done
>for 40 years or more.

It only seems sequential because that's the way it was done for 40
years. Databases routinely multithread queries that SEEM to be
sequential.

>There may be applications which can actually benefit from
>multi-threading, but 'creating 800,000 threads one-per-word' is not one
>of them

Yes it is. I wouldn't construct more threads than CPUs. Before
launching the 17th, I'd wait for one of the 16 to terminate. In the
end it would run about 12 times faster than a single-threaded
solution.

>It is certainly true that interactive games running on games consoles
>can benefit from multi-threading on multple CPUs and GPUs.

So can business systems.

>> during the '60s, in LISP, Smalltalk and Simula. The idea was largely
>ignored
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>recently.

I not only heard of them, I wrote about them.
```

- **In-Reply-To:** `<b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com>`

```
>> C++ was developed in 1981 and was commonly available (cflow)
>> on Unix in the 80s.

> The correct date is 1983.

Classes were added to C and this was called 'C with Classes' and was
implemented by cflow in 1980 or so.  It was later extended and renamed
C++ in 1983.

> By the late '80s, most PCs had well over one meg accessible via
> EMS.

EMS was a mechanism for copying memory 16Kb at a time.  It required
special hardware to implement the EMS on 8088 and 80286 based machines
as well as the RAM itself.  It is overstating _hugely_ to claim that
'most PCs had well over 1Mb'.

Certainly later machines, such as 386s, could implement EMS in
software, but that is not true of 'most PCs' of the 80s.

"""With an 8086, 8088 or 80286 processor EMS requires a hardware based
memory manager, and so an EMS board is required. 80386 processors and
above are capable of emulating EMS in software."""

> Real memory was no limit on virtual memory systems.

Such as MS-DOS ? No wait that isn't.  Such as Windows 1.  No wait that
isn't either.  Perhaps Windows 2.  No.  Windows 3.0.  Well, yes, but
that is 1990.

"""Windows 3.0, released in May, 1990, was a complete overhaul of the
Windows environment. With the capability to address memory beyond 640K
..."""
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 23)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1109997615.297809.287180@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<dkai21d2r2dhi2o31dkp2rbd20f88egbcf@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109646593.681844.221430@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <dkai21d2r2dhi2o31dkp2rbd20f88egbcf@4ax.com>`

```
> It didn't copy anything. It bank-switched 16K into the
> machine's 1M address space.

Yes, and if you wanted to save something that was in the 0-640Kb memory
area you _copied_ it to the 16Kb EMS address space and then banked
switched it away.  To recover back into the 0-640Kb you bank switched
it to the EMS address and then _copied_ it to the 0-640Kb area.

EEMS on the other hand (such as the AST RAMPage) could bank switch
anywhere.  So typically these systems would strip down to 256Kb on the
motherboad then fill the address space from the RAMPage and could bank
switch the whole memory above 256Kb in about 12 register store
operations.

>> It is overstating _hugely_ to claim that 'most PCs had
>> well over 1Mb'  accessible via  EMS.

> Why? They did.

Almost all 8088 PCs had 640Kb or less. A very few had EMS hardware,
such as the Intel memory cards because they were so expensive.

Certainly a 386 could emulate the EMS access using software, but MS-DOS
did not have that until MS-DOS 5  but that was mid 1991, hardly 'the
late 80s'.  Ceratinly one could use QEMM on a 386.

In the late 80s most existing PCs were still 8088 or 286 based. The
number that had EMS was the relatively small number of Intel EMS cards.
 Many 386s still ran MS-DOS and so the numbers of those with EMS
available was the number of QEMMs sold.
```

```
On 4 Mar 2005 20:40:15 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> It didn't copy anything. It bank-switched 16K into the
>> machine's 1M address space.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>it to the EMS address and then _copied_ it to the 0-640Kb area.

There was no need to copy. Construct the thing in EMS. When you want
to use it, read from EMS.

XMS was the one that copied pages into low memory. XMS used 286
protected mode to access inexpensive motherboard memory.

>>> It is overstating _hugely_ to claim that 'most PCs had
>>> well over 1Mb'  accessible via  EMS.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>such as the Intel memory cards because they were so expensive.

In the late '80s, I worked with graphics-oriented hardware such as
screen adaptors, laser printer and scanner cards. Some was OEM, most
off-the-shelf. For example, the laser printer card had a bit per
pixel. Software painted an 8.5x11 inch graphic image in 8 million
bits, pulled the trigger and ..shazam.. a page was printed in three
seconds. The printer was a bare Canon engine with no logic. The memory
card AND printer engine would have retailed for less than a printer,
about $400 vs. 600 at the time, and it was much faster. 

The scanner worked same way in reverse. A copy machine required two
I/O commands -- scan followed by print. If you wanted to save or OCR
scan the page, a 1M bitmap was sitting in memory.

Intel made expensive EMS cards (RAMPage); other companies made cheap
ones. As the 286 became popular, demand for hardware bank-switching
dried up.

In the early '90s I worked on embedded processors that were still
16-bit. They had EMS within the same chip package as the CPU, along
with everything else on a motherboard. A whole PC-on-a-chip cost well
under $50.

>Certainly a 386 could emulate the EMS access using software, but MS-DOS
>did not have that until MS-DOS 5  but that was mid 1991, hardly 'the
>late 80s'.  Ceratinly one could use QEMM on a 386.

The 80286 was released by Intel in 1982. By the mid-80s, it was fairly
common on desktops. DOS extenders thrived in that world. There were
dozens, if not hundreds, of them available before MSDOS5 and 386s.

>In the late 80s most existing PCs were still 8088 or 286 based. The
>number that had EMS was the relatively small number of Intel EMS cards.
> Many 386s still ran MS-DOS and so the numbers of those with EMS
>available was the number of QEMMs sold.

And PharLap, Blinker, Qualitas (386Max), DRI, Borland ... Even I wrote
a memory manager, so it couldn't have been that difficult.
```

```
> There was no need to copy. Construct the thing in EMS. When you want
> to use it, read from EMS.

EMS was only a 16Kb window, if objects > 16Kb were required then it was
necessary to keep hammering the EMS board to change the window.  The
overhead on this could be very high and thus the code ran very slowly.
It was often faster to copy to real memory so that it could be accessed
much faster.

>Intel made expensive EMS cards (RAMPage)

Intel did not make the RAMPage, AST did.  RAMPage was not EMS, it was
EEMS.  Intel cards were expensive, so were AST RAMPage, I still have
both types here.

> The 80286 was released by Intel in 1982. By the mid-80s, it was
fairly
> common on desktops.

The first IBM AT with a 80286 was late 1984:

"""IBM 5170 AT ("Advanced Technology"). Released August 14, 1984.
"""

Now the exact 'middle' of the 80s is the end of 1984, your claim is
that this was 'fairly common' within 4 months of being announced ?

> DOS extenders thrived in that world.

""" -----------------------------------------------------------
> First clarification:  QEMM does not work on a 286, never worked on a
> 286, and QRAM which was designed for <=286 is no longer available.
>QEMM was designed for 386 & upward,
"""

> And PharLap, Blinker, Qualitas (386Max), DRI, Borland ...

Doesn't the '386Max' give you a clue that these are not for 286
machines ?

DRI, for example was more advanced in memory management than others,
DR-DOS 5 had EMM386 (not for 286) and did have HIDOS.COM and I quote
from the manual (which by the way is Mid 1990, far from 'mid 80s'):

"""This command allows users of Intel 80286-based computers to load DR
DOS kernel into the high memory area above 1 Mb (if this computer has
more than 1 MByte of memory).  It also allows users of computers based
on [list of chipsets] to access the upper memory between 640Kb and 1
MByte to load drivers, TSRs, ...."""

The 80286 in real mode could be tricked into using an extra 64Kb -
16bytes by using the #FFFF segment.  This is _NOT_ EMS.  It was not
available to user programs (though it did allow more free RAM in
0-640Kb.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aidm21hgou71vgfamkg1me67d88auh0mn7@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109646593.681844.221430@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <dkai21d2r2dhi2o31dkp2rbd20f88egbcf@4ax.com> <1109997615.297809.287180@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <lgtj21l02kmmig3ddb7ig07kk82opdm9k3@4ax.com> <1110062063.377794.199030@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <vghk21hue5ut22vb5tm06tkjg5q1sht3sh@4ax.com> <1110083054.585560.165130@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 5 Mar 2005 20:24:14 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> I didn't mention QEMM on 286. There were many DOS extenders that did
>> run on 286.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>None of the 286 'extenders' supplied EMS.

EMS came first, on 8088 machines. When the 286 became popular, there
were quite a few commercial programs, such as Lotus 123, written to
use EMS. To support them, 286 memory managers simulated EMS by using
extended memory above one meg (XMS). 

It's true that the 286 could not provide hardware bank switching, or
mapping, as the 386 could. However EMS could be simulated by
allocating a 64K block in low memory and providing int67h services
that copied 16K chunks of XMS to and from that block.
```

```
>> It only seems sequential because that's the way it was done for 40
>> years.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>number of CPUs available.

Unless a DBA, under a wrong-headed theory like yours, configures the
database to be mono-tasking. I saw one today. In that case one CPU is
maxed out and the other fifteen are idle. Application programmers are
pressed to provide parallelism by dividing the work into multiple
processes (jobs). The database is partitioned coincident with the jobs
and application programmers believe the speed increase came from
partitioning. They don't understand that the real issue is
mono-tasking.
```

```
>>There may be applications which can actually benefit from
>>multi-threading, but 'creating 800,000 threads one-per-word' is not
one
>>of them

>Yes it is. I wouldn't construct more threads than CPUs. Before
>launching the 17th, I'd wait for one of the 16 to terminate. In the
>end it would run about 12 times faster than a single-threaded
>solution.

You are still creating 800,000 new threads. You are still doing (
800,000 x number of instructions to create a thread ) which is not
insignificant. It is still a very poor design.  You are adding huge
overhead which will make it much slower on single CPU and may require
several CPUs before you even break-even.
```

```
> You heard of a thread pool?

Yes, but I saw no evidence that RW was attempting to use one, or even
that they could be used.

In fact, if threading were to be used, it would still be better to put
the words into a queue from which they are taken out by a small number
of threads rather than having the word extraction driving the threads.
Even reusing a pool 800,000 times is likely to be more overhead than
the multi-threading will save.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109801916.110682.41310@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <K8rVd.98080$pc5.39479@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
>> You heard of a thread pool?
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the multi-threading will save.

I think multiple word consuming threads would _certainly_ be more efficient.

One concern then would be the correct management and overhead of ensuring 
each word is pulled only once from the queue.  So perhaps a better solution 
would be to have a small number of threads that handled blocks of text and 
extracted and handled the words sending its results to a result catcher that 
may or may not handle some real time indexing.  The block size could real 
time optimized, the thread pool size could be real time optimized.  This 
would _suck_ for the bible and 800,000 words, but would get better as the 
size of the file increased and the ability to handle everything sequentially 
does hinder the performance.  I would venture a guess that getting into a 
few 100s MB or GB of data it would run better than any of the other options 
people have discussed. Of course bigger than that and you'd be best looking 
at data mining tools.

But anyway, now we're reaching a compromise where we could agree that 
perhaps RW's thread solution is not so wrong.
Point is *every* suggestion or algorithm has merit if is fulfils the task, 
even if you discard it in its entirety it still forces you to think about 
problems in a different manner.

JCE
```

- **Message-ID:** `<1109839384.325806.280570@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109801916.110682.41310@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <K8rVd.98080$pc5.39479@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>`

```
> One concern then would be the correct management and overhead of
ensuring
> each word is pulled only once from the queue.

That is what locks are for.  Only one thread can pull a word from the
queue at a time.

I did write a printer buffering system nearly 20 years ago which used
multi-threading (actually it had to use full processes but running in
the same code and data segments) because it had to monitor multiple
i/o.  It used a circular buffer and had to toggle locks to ensure the
buffer was kept uncorrupted.

> Now we're reaching a compromise where we could agree that
> perhaps RW's thread solution is not so wrong.

As is usual with RW he vastly overstates and hyperbolises.  He states,
for example, that multi-threading will save resources, but it does this
only in the very narrow areas where many CPUs are available and
otherwise unused.  In fact as there will be many more cycles used up,
not just by the extra code but also by the scheduler it will use _more_
resources. 'Saving' resources when nothing else will use them is
pointless.  Wasting resources when other processes are trying to share
them is also pointless.

Most multi-CPU systems are servers and designated to run server
software and multiple streams.  Even desktops are not just running a
single task, not since MS-DOS of 15 years ago.  I have a Linux desktop
for development and while I am typing here 'top' shows 160 tasks
running, though the vast majority are sleeping.

If you have multiple CPUs then run multiple streams. The OS will be
processing the filesystem cache, the database will use multiple CPUs.
The servers that I look after have multiple users and run several X
desktops (to LTSP diskless graphic terminals) plus each user runs
several copies of applications.  Typically there are several hundred
tasks.

You may note that RW's machines are corporate servers (not desktops) on
which he specifically closed down all other services to run his test.

The idea that one program should grab every resource available should
have died with MS-DOS.

There _are_ good reasons for multi-threading, such as having many
threads having to wait for different responses, and this may apply to
database systems or web servers.

RW has made many overstatements about where it can be used and when.

> forces you to think about problems in a different manner.

The hard bit is when RW has his steel trap mind closed around a subject
getting him to think about it in a different manner.
```

```
Don't use a single queue. Create a queue per thread. Think of the
parser as a dealer distributing cards to the multiple players. The
only locks required are on the last card in each pile.

>> Now we're reaching a compromise where we could agree that
>> perhaps RW's thread solution is not so wrong.

>resources. 'Saving' resources when nothing else will use them is
>pointless.  Wasting resources when other processes are trying to share
>them is also pointless.

Whether other processes are trying to share can only be known at
execution time .. and mediated by the operating system. Making
ASSumptions about other processes at design time is the path to
wasting idle resources.

>Most multi-CPU systems are servers and designated to run server
>software and multiple streams.  Even desktops are not just running a
>single task, not since MS-DOS of 15 years ago.  I have a Linux desktop

It shows 160 processes, not threads. Run 'glance' to see how much
multithreading  is going on. Your Java processes will typically show
50-100 threads each.

>You may note that RW's machines are corporate servers (not desktops) on
>which he specifically closed down all other services to run his test.

No I didn't. The other services happened to be idle.

The idea that program designers should second-guess execution-time
workload should have died with single-processor mainframes.

>threads having to wait for different responses, and this may apply to
>database systems or web servers.

Tell it to Java and databases, both of which routinely multithread a
```

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1109902300.711241.169770@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<gbaf21d2ks9lmoo60lopl1bnuuvgumbkf0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109801916.110682.41310@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <K8rVd.98080$pc5.39479@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <1109839384.325806.280570@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <gbaf21d2ks9lmoo60lopl1bnuuvgumbkf0@4ax.com>`

```
> Don't use a single queue. Create a queue per thread. Think of the
> parser as a dealer distributing cards to the multiple players. The
> only locks required are on the last card in each pile.

No. That is just silly.  Threads are not going to run equally so some
may exhaust their queue while others still have many items.  Each queue
will also need a lock to ensure that only one process is updating the
queue front at any one time, which includes the parser, so you have
replaced one lock with many.

If you had studdied queueing theory you would know not to create
multiple queues but would have one queue and multiple servers.

> Every mainframe, server and (soon) desktop is not a very narrow area.

Every mainframe and server is already running more processes and
threads than it has CPUs.

It may well be that many desktops have multiple processors, but these
will be primarily for graphics processing (such as Cell is).  Geeks
will always want to overclock as many CPUs as they can supply Liquid
Nitrogen to, but the future is not bigger fatter more bloated and
unmanagable Windows machines that need more computing power each month
because of the spyware and zombie remailers they have picked up. (and
anyway those extra CPUs have been 'pwned').

'Every desktop (soon)' is provably wrong.  I have desktop machines here
still doing useful tasks that are at least a decade old - eg a 486
running Windows 95, and as it happens I am working on a laptop that I
bought in 1995 and has just been returned by my daughter as she has
upgraded to one only 3 years old (a free discard).

I expect in a decade's time to still be running some of my newer
current machines.  So that is at least one that _won't_ be multi-CPU,
unless it manages to clone itself internally.

> It shows 160 processes, not threads.

Regardless, my CPU has plenty to get on with. I just checked one
client's server: 476 tasks.

> Run 'glance'

That's probably a Sun thing.

> Your Java processes will typically show 50-100 threads each.

Java may well support multi-threading, but that does not mean that it
is automatic.  Whether Java threads is dependent on whether the
application wants to or not.  As it happens I have JBoss running on my
system and it _does_ use multi-threading.  Sensibly. It starts each new
service request off in its own thread.

> The other services happened to be idle.

Ok, granted I may have misunderstood that point. I'll change my point
to "specifically waited until all other services were asleep". But then
you claim to 'saving' resources by using all the CPUs when if you
weren't there they would all be wasted entirely, and in fact were
probably wasted for hours afterwards.  By that measure you should have
written the problem in the most inefficient BASIC so that it would use
the maximum CPU time thus 'saving' more.

The issue is: which is saving resources: ( one CPU x 3 units of time )
or ( 4 CPUs x 1 unit of time ).  Putting it another way if 4 batch
streams can run in a given total time each on a single CPU or 3 batch
streams can run each using 4 CPUs in the same time then which is
'saving' more.

This is not to say that multi-threading is wrong.  It is just that your
arguments for justifying it aren't fully developed.

> The idea that program designers should second-guess execution-time
> workload should have died with single-processor mainframes.

But that is __EXACTLY__ what __YOU__ are doing. You designed your
program to use 16 CPUs because that is what the machine had.  Yoiu are
'2nd guessing' that all 16 CPUs are always available when your program
runs.  You are guessing that yours will be the only program in the
machine, the same mistake that DOS programmers made.

> Tell it to Java and databases, both of which routinely multithread a
> single transaction.

Databases, yes.  Web Servers yes. As I already stated. And that is
because they need to work on behalf of multiple clients and each task
has an unknown run-time and characteristics.  Each client requires its
work to be done asynchronously (though locks may be used to synchronise
with others).

Java _may_ multi-thread, and does so for such things as garbage
collection, but that does not mean that the application itself is
multi-threaded.

> Remember: the Marine Corps symbol is a bulldog. :)

And the reason that they are called Bulldogs is that they were bred to
fight bulls. Once they get a grip on a bull, no matter where, they
won't let go no matter what.  How appropriate, Robert, once you snap on
an idea, no matter what, you will hang onto it.
```

```
On 3 Mar 2005 18:11:40 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> Every mainframe, server and (soon) desktop is not a very narrow area.
>
>Every mainframe and server is already running more processes and
>threads than it has CPUs.

It's impossible to run more than 100%. Servers typically run 50% CPU
utilization; mainframes run about 75%.

>'Every desktop (soon)' is provably wrong.  I have desktop machines here
>still doing useful tasks that are at least a decade old - eg a 486
>running Windows 95, and as it happens I am working on a laptop that I
>bought in 1995 and has just been returned by my daughter as she has
>upgraded to one only 3 years old (a free discard).

A free discard is telling. The world is full of free or inexpensive
discards. My home desktop is a corporate discard I bought last year
for $150, including a legal W2K. A few years hence I'll replace it
with a multi-CPU corporate discard. 

>I expect in a decade's time to still be running some of my newer
>current machines.  So that is at least one that _won't_ be multi-CPU,
>unless it manages to clone itself internally.

Why? Better machines will be available for very little money.

>> Run 'glance'
>
>That's probably a Sun thing.

I thought it was a open source thing but now see it's an HP thing.
Glanceplus, one word. It's an improved  'top'. The GUI version has
many goodies.

>The issue is: which is saving resources: ( one CPU x 3 units of time )
>or ( 4 CPUs x 1 unit of time ).  Putting it another way if 4 batch
>streams can run in a given total time each on a single CPU or 3 batch
>streams can run each using 4 CPUs in the same time then which is
>'saving' more.

A batch job can run in parallel two ways:
1. Change the APPLICATION logic to run multiple processes. For
example, process 1 handles customers beginning with 0-2, process 2
handles 3-5, etc. This is now we distributed workload to multiple
clerks before computers.

2. Run multiple threads within a single process. This is how Java and
databases do it.

>> The idea that program designers should second-guess execution-time
>> workload should have died with single-processor mainframes.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>machine, the same mistake that DOS programmers made.

There are ways to make the program heuristic. There are threads
libraries such as the excellent one from Sun that automatically
suspend surplus threads until a processor is available. 

>> Tell it to Java and databases, both of which routinely multithread a
>> single transaction.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>with others).

Batch programs are also a yes. Today I worked on a single embedded SQL
statement more than 500 lines long. It had sub-selects within
sub-selects, each candidates for parallelism. They were doing Full
Table Reads on millions of rows. The whole thing ran in three seconds.
For grins I forced it to mono-task. It ran seven minutes.

>> Remember: the Marine Corps symbol is a bulldog. :)
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>an idea, no matter what, you will hang onto it.

True, but my preference is for sight hounds -- dogs designed for speed
and having the ballerina look. My love was a granddaughter of the all
time prize winning Borzoi: Majenkur Gyrfalcon. She could do a mile in
under two minutes, and frequently did running behind my scooter. She
stood 30 inches tall at the shoulder (more then most Great Danes) yet
weighed only 75 pounds. 

She was SO beautiful, but couldn't win conformation trials. Why,
because she wasn't 'exotic', the European look preferred by judges,
contrary to the published Breed Standard they're supposed to be using.
Her coat was silky, the Standard calls for silky, but judges give
ribbons to wiry because they liked it better. Judging dogs is like
programming in that newer=sexier prevails over classic form. When it's
all subjective, it's about ego. Standards were supposed to have
eliminated that. They failed.
```

```
:>It's impossible to run more than 100%. Servers typically run 50% CPU
:>utilization; mainframes run about 75%.

Your typical MVS/zOS mainframe runs well over 90%.

MVS is able to handle it. Unix still cannot.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2005-03-03T07:08:44-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<112dvo0oaqa5e8@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109801916.110682.41310@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <K8rVd.98080$pc5.39479@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>`

```

"jce" <defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com...
> "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> I think multiple word consuming threads would _certainly_ be more
efficient.

With a single thread, partitioning the input file may be more
efficient for larger files, than processing the whole file, at once.

There is a n * log (n) factor for sorting, where n is the number
of unique words. Since words with small word counts are
spread throughout the file, partitioning into say 10 parts
reduces the size to, for example, 10 * ((n * u) * log (n * u)),
where u is a factor, less than 1.0 to account for the probability
that some words with, say small word counts, will not occur
in some partitions.

When I compared the result of running a single 53MB file
(7 982 815 words, 107 462 unique) versus a 10-partition
53MB file, the results were 24.77s versus 19.39s.
However, with a 5MB file (789 781 total, 12 691 unique),
the process was slower, 0.60s versus 0.94s. This is likely
the result of having to merge the parts to get the totals and,
also, the overhead of the process. When the 5MB file was
processed with a minimum partition of 5MB the result was
0.71s, thus 0.11s for overhead.

[A word of caution. The processing was done with a 16-bit
program. The sort (quicksort) had to transfer data to and
from an extended memory emulator. While the sort speed
was improved by using a "heap funnel" (Wirth), the savings
for a 32-bit program may not be as great.]

For the 53MB file, unique words per partition were:
 1: 30 452
 2: 28 679
 3: 29 079
 4: 17 706
 5: 62 052
 6: 53 120
 7: 54 403
 8: 53 428
 9: 53 874
10: 24 404
As may be seen, each was much less than the 107 462 for
the entire file, thus improving the speed of the sort.

A multi-threaded solution probably should not attempt to
break the text into words; but rather to provide each thread
with the starting and ending positions in the text file and allow
the thread to do its own parsing.
```

- **References:** `<0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109801916.110682.41310@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <K8rVd.98080$pc5.39479@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <112dvo0oaqa5e8@corp.supernews.com>`

```
On Thu, 3 Mar 2005 07:08:44 -0500, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
wrote:

>With a single thread, partitioning the input file may be more
>efficient for larger files, than processing the whole file, at once.
>
>There is a n * log (n) factor for sorting, where n is the number
>of unique words. 

Usually, but not always. I demonstrated a sorting technique --
radix/insert -- that ran in  kN + 2N+, where k is the length of the
first-pass key in bytes and the + following 2N is for close-range
rearrangements by the Insertion pass. Log(n) means log2(n).

In practice, applying radix to the first three bytes of a fifteen byte
random key produced sort time five times faster than n * log(n)
(quicksort).

>Since words with small word counts are
>spread throughout the file, partitioning into say 10 parts
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>in some partitions.

Correct conclusion for the wrong reason. Most of the program's time
will be consumed searching for common words, not distinct words.
Distribution of common words will be the same for each of the 10
partitions. 

>When I compared the result of running a single 53MB file
>(7 982 815 words, 107 462 unique) versus a 10-partition
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>the thread to do its own parsing.

How does it determine the starting and ending positions? To do that,
it has to parse a small region of the file, near the boundary. Before
parsing, it has to convert everything to upper case and convert
quote-s to s-space. These pre-parsing steps require the whole file be
in memory. That's ok for a 5MB file, but may cause paging for 50MB
that would negate all the saving.

I think a *general* solution would single-thread parsing and
multi-thread indexing.
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:0rcf219ctust9604jgd37icoaj2fsdq9fu@4ax.com...
> On Thu, 3 Mar 2005 07:08:44 -0500, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> (quicksort).

Random keys work well in theory because they tend
to be evenly distributed; but words are not so evenly
distributed, thus it is not possible to guarantee good
performance in the insertion pass.

MSD radix sort, as described by Sedgewick, gets its
speed by doubling the space requirement, essentially
no in-place swapping. Yes, I know that pointers may
be used, and I have space for them; by the key-index
counting is the killer.

Sedgewick also states, "In conventional programming
environments, the inner loop of the key-index counting
program on which radix sorts are based contains a
substantially higher number of instructions than do the
inner loops of quicksort and mergesort. This property
of the implementations implies that the sublinear
methods that we have been describing may not be as
much faster that quicksort (say) as we might expect
in many situations."

While MSD radix is out of the question, I might choose
to try LSD radix on MSD characters--just out of
curiosity.

> >Since words with small word counts are
> >spread throughout the file, partitioning into say 10 parts
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> partitions.

Because hashing is used, the cost of an insert or word count
increment is near 1. It is, as I explained, sort that has n * log (n)
cost.

[snip]
> >A multi-threaded solution probably should not attempt to
> >break the text into words; but rather to provide each thread
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> that would negate all the saving.

It need only read a block at the planned boundary, then
search for the next "white space" character, say space or
new line (line feed). Once the file size is obtained, only
nine reads (for ten partitions) and the scan of a few
characters is required to find the ending position.

> I think a *general* solution would single-thread parsing and
> multi-thread indexing.
```

- **References:** `<0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109801916.110682.41310@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <K8rVd.98080$pc5.39479@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <1109806756.736865.323350@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <0pyVd.127139$qB6.83654@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <112dvo0oaqa5e8@corp.supernews.com> <0rcf219ctust9604jgd37icoaj2fsdq9fu@4ax.com> <112frc8jh29h525@corp.supernews.com>`

```

"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
news:112frc8jh29h525@corp.supernews.com...
>
> "Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
> news:0rcf219ctust9604jgd37icoaj2fsdq9fu@4ax.com...
[snip]
> > Correct conclusion for the wrong reason. Most of the program's time
> > will be consumed searching for common words, not distinct words.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> cost.

It seems there were problems with both the search and
the sort.

I replaced the virtual heap file with a table and quicksort
would no longer run; so I replaced quicksort with heapsort.
The program ran 6s faster, about 18.85s.

Thinking there was room for improvement in the sort, I
separated the two processes (loading the table and sorting it)
by creating a file of unique words so that I could test different
sorts.. It took 17.30s to create the unsorted file. I verified the
sort time by loading the file into a heap sort program, the sort,
alone, ran in 1.37s.

Knowing there was a problem in the early process, it quickly
occurred to me that the scatter table (hashing with separate
chaining) was not large enough. It had too few linked lists to
handle the number of unique words. The linked lists were
getting too long and slowing the search. I increased the scatter
table from 8K to 64K entries and the table creation part ran
in about 12.5s.

With the changes incorporated into one program, it now runs
in 13.89s, or about 11s faster than before.

Depending on one's definition of "common words," I would
have to say it was not "common words," which were located
early in the linked lists; but, rather, other frequently occurring
words.

It has been an interesting exercise and, if someone can produce
a 500MB file with 1 000 000 unique words (is that possible?),
I am ready.
```

```
On Thu, 03 Mar 2005 06:46:20 GMT, "jce" <defaultuser@hotmail.com>
wrote:

>I think multiple word consuming threads would _certainly_ be more efficient.

Thank you.

>One concern then would be the correct management and overhead of ensuring 
>each word is pulled only once from the queue.  So perhaps a better solution 
>would be to have a small number of threads that handled blocks of text and 
>extracted and handled the words sending its results to a result catcher that 
>may or may not handle some real time indexing.  

The indexing (and duplicate detection) is what's being multithreaded.
The fastest technique is going to be a tree, as opposed to hashing.
Also, a tree will sort the words, as required? by the problem
definition; a hash will not.

Trying to multithread parsing presents problems of partitioning input
on word boundaries. It's somewhat a chicken-and-egg problem. Moreover,
most time will be spent indexing, not parsing.

>The block size could real 
>time optimized, the thread pool size could be real time optimized.  This 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>at data mining tools.

Which get their speed from multithreading.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 23)_

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>
- **Date:** 2005-03-03T18:32:48+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<q5me2115srhn87b4sq9peprs71lr7oj89e@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <1109272803.722372.291640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <1109545834.352959.324880@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <b8f7211t596tfs5jmobd3a8iql8r2rll3d@4ax.com> <1109801916.110682.41310@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <K8rVd.98080$pc5.39479@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>`

```
The Thread Pool _Service_ is available only to OO programs running on
Windows. On other platforms, you have to do it yourself. 

The multithreading demo I posted here did its own thread reuse by
putting requests into queues. On a Solaris machine with four CPUs, it
ran three times as fast as the single-threaded version.



On Wed, 02 Mar 2005 22:31:06 GMT, "jce" <defaultuser@hotmail.com>
wrote:

>You heard of a thread pool?
>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 21)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-03-02T11:47:57-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1109792877.612586.204690@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<3dv9219cr52rd6s32psbctluihf1kio839@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <cvv9kv$p2d$1@panix5.panix.com> <3dv9219cr52rd6s32psbctluihf1kio839@4ax.com>`

```
> There was no Y2K problem in the worlds of PC and Unix because they
generally
> used a four digit year from the beginning.

That is not even close.  Unix and C do not use '4 digit year' nor '2
digit year'. They use a date and time format 'time_t' that is a 32 bit
int.  The maximum year value that can be held is 2038.  So in 33 years
time they will have a crisis that will require changing their data
formats.

One of the common ways to process dates is to convert to a struct tm
and then use printf to output with a format of, say, "%02d/%02d/%02d".

In a struct tm the tm_year field has a value or the year since 1900.
Thius in the year 2000 the date would be output as: 02/02/100

Many sites recognised this and remedied it, some did not.

PC software did have significant Y2K issues.  In many cases the upgrade
cycle catered for getting new versions of programs to customers.

"""Windows 98 Year 2000 Update : [Windows 98, Y2K]
This component is part of the Windows 98 Customer Service Pack. This
update
corrects several minor issues associated with generating dates on your
computer ...
"""
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message 
news:3dv9219cr52rd6s32psbctluihf1kio839@4ax.com...

> Management STILL hasn't learned the $200B lesson. It permitted most
> Y2K fixes to use a fixed window of 10-30 years, thereby guaranteeing
> another crisis 10-30 years hence. It should have insisted on a sliding
> window, which would have cost nothing,  if not a more expensive four
> digit year.

Just to take some heat from RW :-)

Maybe the $200B lesson was that world, and particularly, the US, is thriving 
on consulting fees.
Some Y2K activities simple did not warrant the level of attention that they 
got.  I don't have specific evidence for this, but in my time alive during 
1999 in a LARGE GLOBAL company I did not once see a business case for 
anything related to Y2K.

There were two factors that I _did_ see.

If we screw it up, it's embarrassing - particularly as we are selling 
massive amounts of consulting services, and products related to Y2K.
We have to fix it because if we don't then who knows what could happen? we 
might have every machine in the county seize and stop working, the world 
will vaporize, the buildings will fall down, we will get stuck in an 
elevator for years.

The best thing that came out of Y2k was ESPN's "Follow me to Freedom" 
commercial.

The $200B lesson.  The cheapest solution is thes solution that you don't 
need.

I do not _DENY_ that there were criticial Y2K issues, but the "panic" was 
the business driver, not any rationality.  Internal reports did not need to 
say 2000 as it was freaking obvious what 00 was......the fact that you hired 
consultant developers at $150 /hr to change the code, write test cases, 
execute test cases - after screwing around with some Tick Tock utility that 
you had to pay for - was not relevant, because we weren't Y2K 
compliant......

As far as I recall, in February 1999, Italy as a whole had a terribly 
negative Gartner group Y2K rating.  The British govt warned about flying 
over Italy at that time due to aviation Y2K concerns.
I don't recall off hand anything happening to Italy that would make national 
news.  Parmalat was something different :-)

Overall, I don't off hand recall a single Y2K of merit -other than a $90,000 
rental bill from BlockBuster which they no doubt fixed with a $6 /hr 
employee.  Yes, I know, it was all down to you diligent and hard working 
times that slaved over code ensuring that the world was a safer place.....

I swear to your God if you have one that if I am asked to participate in a 
Y2K30 project I will either (a) Stick a sharp stick in my eye to confirm 
whether what I've been doing really is better than that or (b) Take the 
money and celebrate - I missed the gravy train being a "regular" exempt 
employee back then...even my overtime was free.

JCE
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

```
> but human beings would have missed scheduled reviews
> and raises, or not retired when  entitled.

One of the reasons that Y2K was overhyped was that claims were made
about systems failing when they had already solved the problems decades
before.

For example a future date, such as a scheduled review, or a retirement,
or an endowment falling due had to have its date set in advance.  A 30
year mortgage in 1970 would have needed to have the system process this
termination date correctly.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>

```
On the other hand, I am aware of a Very Costly Y2K bug that cropped up in
the automated parts receiving system for a Major Overseas automobile
manufacturer some while before 12/31/1999.   Parts with long shelf lives
(like engine blocks and fenders) started being rejected by the receiving
computer systems because their expiration dates appeared to have passed;
several shiploads of parts got automatically shipped back overseas before
somebody figured out what was happening and corrected the problem.  (Not, so
far as I know, one of our customers, by the way!).

    -Chuck Stevens
```
