- **In-Reply-To:** `<1109820747.633971.9120@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<tcra11dep0bl1lkb9el57jreju1utc14u0@4ax.com> <0rhv11l119nv0h7qkv4jtsc6sldkebkkd2@4ax.com> <cvpr9p$4jt$1@panix5.panix.com> <cjb421loslu5s3t9r5psik0749oppc44ki@4ax.com> <cvv9kv$p2d$1@panix5.panix.com> <3dv9219cr52rd6s32psbctluihf1kio839@4ax.com> <fYoVd.124191$qB6.58353@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <c9kc21d2t4me4qbuvpsliic44dah6kv5k8@4ax.com> <1109820747.633971.9120@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
> 
> Your opinion is of no consequence.  The managers of airports, traffic
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Completely off the topic you were discussing with your erstwhile friend, 
but your above comment triggered what I was watching on TV last night.

PBS 'Frontline' excellent low-key factual reporting, no histrionics, 
with a comforting voice-over from a narrator, covered the Swiss Air 111 
(McDonnell Douglas MD-11) disaster where the plane crashed into the sea 
off Canada's East Coast at Peggy's Cove in 1998. Crew and passengers 
killed - 250.

It was chilling to watch. Both Canadian and US Aviation authorities, the 
latter also being responsible for setting international standards have 
been investigating for years. And it took *years* for the Canadians to 
retrieve the wreckage dispersed in mini-pieces, some no more than a 
bracket or about three to four inches of wire. As best they could they 
reassembled the frame of the aircraft, but still left with a warehouse 
full of boxes of different parts.

First, fairly common with most large commercial aircraft - some 150 
miles of electrical wiring. Like it or not the wiring deteriorates, 
movement, vibration, age etc. So you get bared wires. Any condensation 
kicking about and it will arc. Now all this wiring just isn't bare, it's 
covered with insulation - don't know what it is - but think of something 
like a lining on a bedspread which in turn is covered with a silvery 
surface - you techies get what I'm trying to describe. Of course the 
wiring and insulation is set by aviation standards.

To summarize - they the Canadians/Americans have reasonably concluded 
this is what happened. Somewhere in the plane, and in overhead areas not 
so particular - who tries to crawl between the outer skin and the cabin 
internal roof - an arc occurred in some wiring, and that approved 
insulation material burned like a paper tissue used to wipe your nose !

The flight recorder box picked up messages from the aircrew when they 
indicated an emergency wanting to land at Halifax, subsequently turned 
to "Immediately". It would appear the cockpit was the prime area 
enveloped in smoke and soot. Instruments went and literally the pilot 
and co-pilot just had no idea of which way was up. Possibly with a tip 
of a wing, the plane nose-dived into the sea.

Back to that insulation. Teams tested and proved that it just wasn't 
safe, (with scenes of their test burns on TV), and made recommendations 
it should be replaced. Think of the cost - all the commercial fleets 
using that specific material. In an aviation hearing, the 'commercials' 
  managed to get an extension not to actually have to implement the 
change until June 2005.

So my wife says, 'You stand more chance of being killed crossing the 
road, rather than dying in an air crash'. Hmmm. Perhaps when I visit my 
daughter back East I should consider a pony and trap - would take a bit 
longer though.

I confirmed the aircraft make from googling - so anybody out there 
interested in aviation - seems to be quite a bit on Swiss Air 111.

Jimmy
```

```
Reminds me of a great movie scene:

Narrator: A new car built by my company leaves somewhere traveling at 60 
mph. The rear differential locks up. The car crashes and burns with everyone 
trapped inside. Now, should we initiate a recall? Take the number of 
vehicles in the field, A, multiply by the probable rate of failure, B, 
multiply by the average out-of-court settlement, C. A times B times C equals 
X. If X is less than the cost of a recall, we don't do one.

Business Lady on Plane: Are there a lot of these kinds of accidents?

Narrator: You wouldn't believe.

Business woman on plane: Which car company do you work for?

Narrator: A major one.


There are many twists to this:

First there is the new Dodge model (that I approve of) which sends out a 
letter - by the way, we do have a minor problem with suspension in the rear 
of your truck that affects high mileage vehicles. Rather than do a recall 
we're extending the warranty.

Second there is the VW model (and this is MY personal story that I share so 
you can do the same)

Me: They say the heater core on my VW needs replacing.  The car has less 
than 35,000 miles.  Can you why I have to pay $800 of labor for something so 
soon in the cars life ?
Customer Service: Hmmm....let me see. [wait] So you say the heater core is 
the problem. What model car?
Me: It's the Jetta that you put glue together in Mexico.  This is really 
pathetic, I'm going to sell it and never buy anything from you again.
Customer Service: Have your dealer call us, we'll take care of the labor 
charge.

Two things I learnt -
1) They only recall using the equations in Fight Club.  They have a status 
that is "watch list" which means it's not on recall, but they are aware of 
problems.  This is why they paid the labor. ALWAYS call your dealer before 
major work :-D
2) Dealers will hate you because the manufacturers do not pay the same labor 
rate you and I would :-)


JCE
```

```
jce wrote:
> 

> 
> Narrator: A new car built by my company leaves somewhere traveling at 60
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 


There is good reason to believe that exactly this scenario happens all
over the place.  You may recall a train crash in England - near London -
a few years ago where one train t-boned another - apparently because the
controlling signal light did not turn red when it was set to go red.  It
came out afterwards that a calculation had been done comparing the cost
of keeping all the signals and control systems fully repaired and up to
snuff to the cost of compensating victims & families.  On an annual
basis, it was cheaper to compensate, given the anticpated number of
accidents.  So: maintenance got neglected.  People died.  The train
company came out ahead.

"Don't be nervous, Arthur Dent: be very, very afraid ... Arthur Dent!"

PL
```

```
anyway.
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:OjJVd.564541$8l.533130@pd7tw1no...
> Peter Lacey wrote:
>> jce wrote:
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> lost his daughter in that crash.
Unfortunately you cannot personalize the issue.

For interesting discussion:
<start link=========
http://www.crookedtimber.org/archives/000414.html

  Basic Trolley Case: There is a run away trolley car careening down some 
railway tracks towards two tunnels. If it stays on the track it is on, it 
will kill five people working in the eastern tunnel. If you pull a switch it 
will move onto a side track, go through the western tunnel and kill the 
person working in it. Assume you can't do anything to stop the car or change 
its path except pull this switch. You pull the switch, saving the five and 
leading to the one's death.

  Fatman: Same runaway car, but without the switch. This time, you're 
standing on a cliff looking over the track the car is about to careen down. 
If unchecked it will kill the five people in the tunnel. Fortunately, there 
is a very fat man standing beside you - fat enough that if somehow he were 
to fall onto the track his sheer mass would stop the trolley. You give him a 
little shove, he falls off the cliff onto the train tracks is killed by the 
trolley (if not the fall) and the five are saved.

A significant number of people think your action in the first case is 
permissible, perhaps even mandatory, but the action in the second case is 
impermissible.

===========end link>

The fact is that there are estimated to be 6,422,192,930 people in the world 
and if we were to ask the US or Europe to spend just ONE dollar per person 
per day helping all of them there would be outcry.  Maybe you're unusual but 
your statement that is "calamity involving the death of just ONE human 
being - then the cost to remedy that is immaterial".  The value of life is 
infinite at its source and dimishes exponentially as you move 
outwards....that's why we get headlines like:  Tsunami hits- 200 000 die, 
supermodel hurt.

If I said cut off your finger and your family will remain safe, you'd do it. 
If I said cut off your finger and I will prevent the massacre of millions in 
Rwanda, you might, but I know you'd think about it even though if the value 
of ONE life is as important as you say it should be millions times easier to 
make the second decision.

I don't refuse you your right to be self righteous in the matter :-) It's 
easier to say "I value all life" than appear cold and say "yeah, whatever, I 
feel sorry for the families but means nothing to me".

JCE
```

```
jce wrote:
> "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
> news:OjJVd.564541$8l.533130@pd7tw1no...

> Unfortunately you cannot personalize the issue.

I tend to disagree with you on that one. In the real world I think the 
aviation or railway official is going to take it very personally - and 
have grievous doubts about his previous position, based on a dollar and 
cents evaluation. Be honest - ask yourself the same question. But before 
you answer it, your family unit and love of same have to have significance.
> 
> For interesting discussion:
> <start link=========
> http://www.crookedtimber.org/archives/000414.html
<snip>

Yes understand what you are illustrating. It's a trick the UK military 
use, and probably also your own, when evaluating candidates as potential 
officer cadets. The test is a 'What if....".

Scenario - eight people in a lifeboat, famous actress, brilliant 
scientific professor, Mom and little kid, an internationally renowned 
entrepreneur etc..... You get the idea.

Only enough food to feed seven out of the eight. Which one do you toss 
overboard. Having got you all to blab on your choice, then they zing 
you. Now chuck # 2 overboard. (Perhaps says one, "If I'd know you wanted 
us to throw two overboard, I would have picked a pair. The mother and 
the kid " :-) ). Seriously, depending on how they evaluate these tests, 
they might acknowledge that candidate's flip remark as a 'positive'.

How effective that particular technique is I don't know - but the intent 
  is valid in a military environment where you are looking for 'war 
leaders/warriors', who can dispassionately make cool judgments. I read 
just last week, "Having made decisions like destroying the French Fleet 
etc., Churchill agonized over some of his decisions"

> 
> ===========end link>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> per day helping all of them there would be outcry. 

Funnily enough, last night on current series, or perhaps re-runs on 
Mondays and Tuesday, the fictional 'West Wing' made that point. 
President Bartlett :-

Nobody gives a toss about foreign policy, just how taxes affect them in 
their pocket book, relating to expense here at home. But we should be 
concerned.

Perhaps I relate to that because Prez Martin Sheen and I are both liberals.


  Maybe you're unusual but

I do know that I'm neither brilliant nor a rocket scientist. But I don't 
think I'm 'unusual' - got to be some that think along the same lines.

> your statement that is "calamity involving the death of just ONE human 
> being - then the cost to remedy that is immaterial".  The value of life is 
> infinite at its source and dimishes exponentially as you move 
> outwards....that's why we get headlines like:  Tsunami hits- 200 000 die, 
> supermodel hurt.

Plus of course the TV networks having swamped us in their own version of 
a Tsunami - it isn't newsworthy anymore. Even with the backing of George 
Bush Senr. and Bill Clinton, I'd bet the relief effort will just fade away.
> 
> If I said cut off your finger and your family will remain safe, you'd do it. 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> feel sorry for the families but means nothing to me".

"Self righteous' - kind of a zinger. When you break it down the word 
'righteous' on its own doesn't indicate a stiff-neck. Howz about 'Just 
somebody who cares ?"

There's a tremendous amount of self-interest in our Western culture. We 
nod awkwardly, acknowledging the Third World is a problem, but don't do 
anything about it. History has its own way of shaping itself, and some 
things come back to bite you. Out of self-interest the coastal European 
nations became empire-builders. Real big problem now - Africans, Arabs 
and Asians in droves becoming major groups in some countries. UK for 
example, since the post WW2 emigration, is now going through a second 
phase of "Let's get out of here and go to Australia and New Zealand". 
Others head for N. America. That's the same situation in the Netherlands.

Coming over here - long term, that's like jumping out of the frying pan 
into the fire. It's not just one guy called Osama, (fairly common name), 
who looks at N. America with a jaundiced eye.

.... Now back to my Treeview - and comment on your observations about 
components......

Jimmy
```

```
people waiting in line for crowns, hip replacements, eye surgery it isn't.

that it was too bad, we have a funding crisis.
```

```
jce wrote:
> "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 

I hate the messages which keep rolling on through previous answers. So 
just taking your point about health care. Subtle, but slightly different 
case.

The one you addressed was the cost of having health care. Has been for a 
long time and probably will remain a contentious issue. If a health 
administrator puts priorities on how he spends his budget, yes, without 
direct intervention, he is aware somebody is going to go short - and 
possibly die. He gets a fixed amount of money for the pie, so he and 
advisers, have to decide how the pie is going to be served as slices.

That's a little bit different to the plane/train examples. I'm 
reasonably certain the latter is now 'privatized' in UK and doesn't go 
by the old name I used - British Rail.

They are in the profit making business, else why exist. New topic for 
industry - ethics. Some have taken a jump at it with sincerity; probably 
majority are still giving it the look over, perhaps not to be followed 
by an serious intent. As the saying goes, "Power corrupts...." but howz 
about "Money corrupts much more quickly ". Even in 'uncomfortable mode' 
and making a decision to avoid spending on safety, having made that 
shaky step froward, is it really that far removed, gradually stepping, 
to arrive at the ultimate temptation which is big time - Bernie Ebbers 
at Worldcom and Kenneth Lay at Enron. (BTW Bernie is an Albertan from 
Edmonton ).

The other issue with health care, that you made passing reference to, 
how you handle the case of a premature birth. This one probably 
generates more heat than the funding of health care. Your own stance, 
religious beliefs, or other beliefs, that construct your own moral code. 
In brief, my thoughts are, only prolong life where you can also give 
somebody 'quality of life'.

There are lots of old folk shuffled off to hospices/homes - to rot, lose 
their marbles and eventually just fade away. Not my choice - as they 
wheel me in on a gurney, tip the bloody thing over so that I croak !

Better still, if still healthy or semi-healthy leave me at home to do my 
own thing, wrapped in the comfort of the memories associated with my own 
home and garden. Then someday my son phones to check on the old guy. No 
reply. Only half-concerned but comes around to check. The old guy has 
gone. Oh how I hate the obits that are worded, "Our beloved...... PASSED 
AWAY......".

The last scenario is absolutely false - from time to time I have to 
remind my wife that we have an unwritten agreement. I worded it, of 
course. "I'm going FIRST. There is no goddamn way I am going to spend 6 
months clearing all her junk out of our basement !"

Jimmy
```

```
I suppose "righteous" is when God tells you what to be do, and "self righteous"
is when you figure it out for yourself.    Except I would think it's a good
thing to figure it out for yourself, and "self righteous" is always an insult.
 I'm moving towards the direction that "righteous" is the worst insult though,
as we find more righteous people strapping explosives to their bodies and
heading out to the market to punish the unrighteous.

When you're righteous, then you can commit any atrocity, and the victims deserve
it.
```

```
On  2-Mar-2005, "jce" <defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:

> Narrator: A new car built by my company leaves somewhere traveling at 60
> mph. The rear differential locks up. The car crashes and burns with everyone
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> X. If X is less than the cost of a recall, we don't do one.

To some extent, we have to accept this type of valuation.   How much is a life
worth?   Is it worth enough to have a world-wide speed limit of 20mph, and as
soon as a kid gets run over, lower it to 5mph?    Vehicles and laws can *always*
be made safer.    And programming design and testing procedures can always be
made more thorough.

The variable is when we have done enough.   The consensus (which is always a
compromise) varies over time.    It also varies depending on what we can afford.
```

```
James J. Gavan wrote:

> The flight recorder box picked up messages from the aircrew when 
> they indicated an emergency wanting to land at Halifax, subsequently 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> with a tip of a wing, the plane nose-dived into the sea.

I have seen a similar documentary on this crash but they told a 
slightly different story.

The stories match as to the reason for the crash (ie electrical
fire made worse by the fact that flammable insulation was also 
used in the fuselage), but it was not the smoke and the lack of 
visibility that brought the plane down.

The reason for the crash was the fire started as you describe, the 
crew detect the smoke and the captain, who liked to fly by the book
immediately went into the aircrew emergency checklist procedures. 

Some 19 minutes later, the fire that was being fed by the outside 
air rushing over the fuselage, was now so intense it destroyed all
remaining controls lines, hence forcing the plane to crash.

The tradgedy is that had the aircrew reacted a little faster they may 
well have been able to land the plane before the controls failed. In 
fact recall that the documentary said one of the recommendations from 
the crash investigation was the emergency checklist procedures should 
be changed to effectively read "if there is any hint of fire land the 
plane ASAP".

Here are a couple of links about the crash:

  http://www.ldc.upenn.edu/Projects/TDT3/topic.research/topic3016.html
  http://members.aol.com/papcecst/Flight111.html

Jussi Jumppanen
Author of: Zeus for Windows (New version 3.94 out now)
"The C/C++, Cobol, Java, HTML, Python, PHP, Perl programmer's editor"
Home Page: http://www.zeusedit.com
```

```
On Thu, 24 Feb 2005 11:02:12 -0800, "Chuck Stevens"

>process is done.

>> I avoid the ALLOCATE verb, available on Micro Focus and

>standards committee nor the implementation.

>> In its place I use C's malloc(),
>
>Available in which COBOL standard?  According to what *standard* calling
>methodology?

By packaging malloc as an external called program, it can be written
in Cobol or C without changing anything in the main programs. 

Calling methodology is implementor-defined. On most platforms it's a
non-issue; it's the same for all languages.

>> If you have a PRACTICAL solution that works on more than the three
>> best-selling compilers, I'm listening.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>extensions to standard COBOL?

Tables/arrays must have an arbitrary size limit. The only limitless
structure in 85 Cobol is a file, which can be impractically slow.

>As to "best-selling compilers", our *best selling* COBOL compiler is
>COBOL74, and to try to get our customer base to move to COBOL85 we made it
>quite a bit more expensive.  Thus far it hasn't worked.

Most industry-leaving companies are interested in following Best
Practices. Companies hanging onto COBOL74 obviously aren't.
You complete the syllogism.

>A *general* solution works in all COBOL environments.  If it doesn't work
>in, or even isn't appropriate for, *all* COBOL environments, it fails the
>test of being a *general* solution.
>
>Or are you changing the qualifier from "general" to "PRACTICAL" on us?

If it works on 90% of existing compilers AND is supported by the
current standard, it qualifies as general in my thinking.
```

```
>Calling methodology is implementor-defined. On most platforms
> it's a non-issue; it's the same for all languages.

What do you mean by 'most platforms' ? Is Unix one platform or 83 ?  Is
MS-DOS 1-8, DR-DOS 3-7, Windows 1 - XP one platform or 27 ?

> it's the same for all languages.

You show a complete absense of knowledge in this area. You obviously
have no idea about calling conventions at all.

The two traditionally different mechanisms were Pascal and C, C had the
caller clean up the stack, Pascal did it in the routine.  The C
mechanism allowed variable number of parameters. Even within these the
order of parameters and promotion created variations.  C++ added name
mangling which is implemented differently by different compilers.

> If it works on 90% of existing compilers AND is supported by
> the current standard, it qualifies as general in my thinking.

And as you discovered when you read the standard the pointers must be
'based' which your code did not do.  Therefore by your own argument
your code was not 'general'.

It also wasn't a 'general solution' because it did not actually solve
the problem, let alone in a generalised way, it merely read the file
into a simple one way list and left it there.
```

- **References:** `<1108703326.879934.273560@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <vmad111l0h49nmhnh1vcb6mp0gjioki38e@4ax.com> <p2dSd.2905172$f47.522482@news.easynews.com> <cvg5r1$2lgf$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3dpn119ol03uumjqlrj4tctk118pda6lpf@4ax.com> <cvibef$127f$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <2dbq1198emt1p5r3827lfgskmhl90m1bn3@4ax.com> <cvl8bl$30qv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <s1ru11ppgmu71f7hbdooebl8kdekc0f6dk@4ax.com> <1109359081.028346.150010@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <6inv11to80pskulrdhlqjgncqbes894062@4ax.com>`

```
> The calling sequence to a .dll or .so is defined by the operating
system,
> independent of language.

You are now attempting to restrict what you meant by 'Calling
methodology' to that of specific types of calls.  However, you once
again are a wonderful mine of misinformation:

"""Calling DLL's under Windows

Users of ACUCOBOL-GT® under Windows have the ability to call routines
packaged in Dynamic Link Libraries (DLL's) written in almost any
language. To achieve this there are a few things you need to know.

Firstly, there are two calling 'conventions', which specify how
parameters are passed to the DLL, under Windows, the C convention and
the Pascal convention. You can choose which convention to use by
setting the DLL-CONVENTION environment variable, remember you can use
SET ENVIRONMENT to set this. If you're not sure which convention is
used by a DLL just try one. If you've got it wrong you'll know pretty
quickly (can you spell GPF?).

Now we need to know how to call the DLL. If the DLL has only one
'entry-point' i.e. it only has one sub-routine packaged in it, and then
we can just call it by name:

   CALL "MYDLL.DLL" USING PARAM-1, PARAM-2.

The Windows runtime always looks for a file with the '.DLL' extension
if it doesn't find a COBOL program with the specified name, so we could
just say CALL "MYDLL". However we find it's easier to keep track of
what's going on if the extension is specified in the call.

Where the DLL has more than one entry-point we just call the DLL by
name to initialise it, then call the routine by name to run it. Let's
imagine that 'MYDLL.DLL' contains two routines 'routine1' and
'routine2'. We want to call 'routine2', so we code:

   CALL "MYDLL.DLL".
   CALL "routine2" USING PARAM-3.

The final thing we need to know is what format the DLL expects the
parameters to be in. The main thing here is whether it expects to get
the parameters as values or addresses. The default for ACUCOBOL-GT® is
to pass the address, also known as 'by reference'. If the DLL expects a
value, then you must specify BY VALUE on the parameter. Let's imagine
that we're calling 'routine1' which expects a number as its first
parameter and the address of a data item as its second; C programmers
will call this a 'pointer'. Since we've already called 'MYDLL.DLL' we
can just go ahead and call 'routine1':

   CALL "routine1" USING BY VALUE WS-NUMBER, WS-DATA-ITEM.

And that's it! It's really straightforward and opens up many
possibilities in the Windows world.""""

Or perhaps:

"""Q. Why do I get "Bad Calling Convention" when trying to call my VC++
dll from VB?

A. Visual Basic only supports calling functions in Dlls that are
declared with the __stdcall calling convention. This is different from
the default convention in VC++,  __cdecl, which stands for "C' calling
convention. The two are not the same (but that's another topic) and are
not interchangeable. The solution? make sure you declare your dll's
functions as __stdcall.
"""
```

```
>"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message 
>news:s1ru11ppgmu71f7hbdooebl8kdekc0f6dk@4ax.com...
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>definition of "general".

The only difference between 02 syntax and mine is 02's requirement for
a BASED clause in the structure header. The (unimplemented) Standard
does not require 'BASED syntax' to use the dynamic structures, it
permits the old extension syntax: SET ADDRESS OF structure TO pointer.
Thus, old programs can be made compliant by adding two words. No logic
changes are required.
```

```
On Sun, 27 Feb 2005 04:39:04 GMT, "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>So, if you are "right" then tell me ANY Standard that accepts the code that you 
>posted in the thread - without modification.

Standards don't compile code. The three most popular compilers accept
the code I posted without modification. The '02 Standard requires the
addition of two words: BASED pointername. Fujitsu recognizes it, but
the other two do not. If they did, I would have used it.

>If not, then tell me what your CURRENT (revised) definition of "general" is?
>
>You just can't have it both ways.

The essence of the question is dynamic allocation and pointers. Two
words is a technical quibble that doesn't invalidate my general
solution. I'd be in error if the '02 Standard required different
grammar (syntax or lexicon). I think its authors wanted it to, but
reluctantly included the extension syntax SET ADDRESS OF for
compatibility with existing code .. like mine.
```

```
So you agree that by YOUR DEFINITION, the code that you provided does NOT

FYI-
  My personal definition of a "general" solution is one that:

A) conforms to a current (or past) generally implemented Standard (i.e that 
currently supported compilers conforming to the specified Standard exist on 3 or 
more operating systems)

   and

B) the solution does not rely on "operating system specific" API's, "calls" or 
other non-portable non-COBOL features.
```

```
I have NEVER argued against pointers.  (see below for recap of my "bad" 
comments)

What I have (and continue) to say is that *IF* your definition of a "general 
solution" to a problem is that it MUST conform to a current Standard *AND* 
compile on 90% of compilers, then any POINTER solution fails this test.  Once 
you admitted that your original description of "general" (in your words) was in 
ERROR and that what you REALLY meant was that "with minimal changes it conforms 
to a current Standard" (regardless of whether or not THAT Standard is actually 
implemented by anyone - much less many vendors - then I say your code meets your 
NEW definition.

NOTE:
  Again on my comment on "bad".

A) I think that IBM customers do not want to have to pay for development of new 
"BASED" syntax (and - by the way - there are also new rules concerning 
addressability for BASED items in Working-Storage, Local-Storage, vs Linkage 
Section)  when IBM customers find the current syntax FINE for their NON_portable 
code.
   and
B) Most IBM shops that I know of (or at least new of) did not want application 
programmers doing dynamic storage manipulation.  This was left for "more senior" 
programmers and specific "system level" code.

Personally, I think that the ALLOCATE and FREE statements are a reasonable 
enhancement to the COBOL Language and the fact that BASED items can be in a 
variety of sections of the Data Division provides for added functionality. 
However, I don't know of any vendor that has implemented this feature of the '02 
Standard yet.
```

```
On 5 Mar 2005 13:46:29 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> It would be faster to suck in the file with a single
>> read, parse lines yourself (with a SEARCH verb) and make a list of
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>bit-twiddling.

How many Cobol programmers does it take to change a light bulb?
Five. One to handle the bulb, three to say how much better they can do
it, and one to reminisce about how Grace would have done it.
Three. One to change the bulb and two to code-review the change.

What do you call a contract Cobol programmer with a beeper and a
cellphone?
An optimist.

What's another definition of optimist?
A Cobol programmer with a thirty year mortgage.

What's the difference between a dead Cobol programmer in the road and
a dead squirrel in the road?
Skid marks before the squirrel.

What do you call someone who hangs out with programmers?
That Cobol guy.

What do Cobol programmers use for birth control?
Their personalities.

If you drop an RPG programmer and a Cobol programmer off a twenty
story building, which lands first?
Who cares?
The RPG programmer. The Cobol programmer has to stop midway to look up
directions in a manual.

What's the difference between God and a Cobol programmer?
God knows he's not a Cobol programmer.

A Cobol programmer, a C++ programmer, a world-class Java programmer
and a PERL scripter are standing on four corners of a field. Someone
drops a hundred dollar bill in the center. Who gets it?
The C++ programmer because:
  A Cobol programmer won't go anywhere for only a hundred dollars.
  There's no such thing as a world-class Java progrmmer.
  The PERL guy can't figure out what's going on.
```

```
M/F - again probably a 10 year or more stint from Don Schricker who is 
now the chairman for both J4 and WG4.

Apart from F/J they are still at it with Chuck for Unisys, Jeff for HP,
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3o2i21l4dnbd1c9qkb4aoh9prsu10tk299@4ax.com...

> Why are Hitachi,
> Fujitsu, LegacyJ, Acucorp and Kobol not represented?

That's a question only the management of Fujitsu, LegacyJ, and Kobol to
answer;  unless senior management at those companies is actively
participating in this forum, anything that might appear here as to their
motives is, and of necessity must be, sheer speculation unless someone has
certain knowledge.

Hitachi has actively supported ITSCJ participation in J4; the primary ITSCJ
representative on J4 is an employee of that company.  ITSCJ fairly recently
became an advisory member; the primary (sponsored by Hitachi) and alternate
(sponsored by Tokyo International University) representatives have expressed
hope to return ITSCJ to active voting status this year.

Acucorp is, and has been for a long time, an advisory member of J4.

The committee encourages, and has continued to encourage, users, vendors,
onsultants, academicians and anybody else with an interest in the future of
COBOL to participate in the standards process, and has done so for my entire
tenure on that committee.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **From:** l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms)
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T08:14:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
.    On  17.02.05
  wrote  riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  1108698281.178522.63150@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com
  about  Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file


r> An actual solution may be:
r>
r>        open infile
r>        until eof:
r>            read infile
r>                  at end set eof
r>                  not at end
r>                        move b to a
r>                        move inrec to b
r>            end-read
r>        close infile

   and so on.

   This solution is OK when the place where the two records from the  
end are to be inserted is only known after reading the whole file.

   Otherwise it might be better to copy the input file only to the  
point where the two records from the end are to be inserted, write the  
rest of the file into a temporary file, and then write the two and get  
the rest from the temporary file.

   The advantage, of course, increases or vanishes with the insertion  
place being near the beginning or the end of the input file.

   The best solution might be to tell the manager to supply the two  
records to be inserted separately.


Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.willms-edv.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

Er liebte hauptsï¿½chlich die Wï¿½rter, die nicht in Wï¿½rterbï¿½chern vorzukommen pflegen. -G.C.Lichtenberg
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: moving data from one place to another in a text file

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T10:24:48-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108751088.579172.247910@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9R7h1wLeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<1108382561.342297.3430@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <6via11hg4sd1k5c3mv33pn8tmusgfdga2l@4ax.com> <1108698281.178522.63150@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <9R7h1wLeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
> Otherwise it might be better to copy the ...

In my view the simpler the program the 'better' it is.  Copying the
file, or parts of it, somewhere else: a temporary file or some
in-memory structure; just complexifies the program and gives more
points where it may be miscoded, or could go wrong.

I doubt that using a temporary file would make the program use less
resources overall (which may be why you thought it 'better'). Reading
the input twice, especially as it is likely to be cached for the 2nd
pass, is probably much faster than opening a new temporary file  to be
written and read back for part of the data.

> The best solution might be to tell the manager to supply the two
> records to be inserted separately.

Which could be easily achieved by having the code you quoted as a
separate program and  added to by writing a and b to a new file.

That would qualify in my view as 'better' because we now have two
programs but each is  simpler.
```
